<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/sch22/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure><p>The potent antioxidant pyranonigrin A, whose production was previously reported in <em>A. niger</em>,<a href="#cit126" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit126">126</a>,<a href="#cit127" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit127">127</a> was found to be biosynthesized by a PKS–NRPS gene cluster different from the <em>pyn</em> cluster.<a href="#cit128" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit128">128</a> The biosynthetic pathway of pyranonigrin A, which was elucidated in <em>Penicillium thymicola</em>, involves initial biosynthesis of the polyketide–nonribosomal peptide product by the PKS–NRPS PyrA, followed by product release catalyzed by either a Dieckmann cyclase (DKC) and/or the hydrolase PyrD (<a href="#sch23" class="usa-link">Scheme 23</a>). Next, FAD-binding monooxygenase PyrC may catalyze epoxidation followed by subsequent ring closure to form the pyrano[2,3-<em>c</em>]pyrrole core, followed by conversion to pyranonigrin S and pyranonigrin A by cytochrome P450 PyrB.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="sch23"><h4 class="obj_head">Scheme 23. Biosynthesis of pyranonigrin A in <em>A. niger</em>.<a href="#cit128" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit128">128</a>.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=6590338_c9md00054b-s23.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/c16d/6590338/767d80d8b5b5/c9md00054b-s23.jpg" loading="lazy" height="246" width="734" alt="Scheme 23"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/sch23/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure></section></section><section id="sec28"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">6. Genetic characterization of secondary metabolites in <em>Aspergillus terreus</em>
</h2>
<p>In 2014, a review summarizing advances in SM genome mining in <em>A. terreus</em> was published by C. J. Guo <em>et al.</em>,<a href="#cit129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit129">129</a> which included the genetic characterization of terretonin,<a href="#cit130" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit130">130</a> asperfuranone,<a href="#cit12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit12">12</a> terrein,<a href="#cit131" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit131">131</a> terreic acid,<a href="#cit132" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit132">132</a> and acetylaranotin.<a href="#cit133" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit133">133</a> This section focuses on discoveries made in <em>A. terreus</em> SM biosynthesis research since that review was published.</p>
<section id="sec29"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">6.1. Biosynthesis of aspterric acid</h3>
<p>The gene cluster responsible for the biosynthesis of the potent herbicide aspterric acid was identified using a resistance-gene-directed approach.<a href="#cit134" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit134">134</a> Scientists focused on identifying SMs that would target the dihydroxyacid dehydratase (DHAD) enzyme within the branched chain amino acid (BCAA) biosynthetic pathway, which is necessary for plant growth and considered a specific target for weed-control agents.<a href="#cit135" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit135">135</a> They reasoned that gene clusters responsible for biosynthesis of a DHAD inhibitor may also include a self-resistance gene, such as an additional copy of DHAD that is not sensitive to the produced inhibitor. Such a cluster was identified in <em>A. terreus</em>, which included genes encoding the sesquiterpene cyclase AstA, cytochrome P450 enzymes AstB and AstC, and a homolog of DHAD AstD. Interestingly, this cluster is conserved across multiple fungal genomes, including <em>Neosartorya fischeri</em> NRRL 181, <em>Penicillium brasilianum</em>, and <em>Penicillium solitum</em> strain RS1.<a href="#cit134" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit134">134</a> To activate the silent gene cluster, <em>astA</em>, <em>astB</em>, and <em>astC</em> were heterologously expressed stepwise in <em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em> RC01, which resulted in the production of the sesquiterpenoid aspterric acid and its biosynthetic intermediates, and enabled researchers to propose the biosynthetic pathway for aspterric acid (<a href="#sch24" class="usa-link">Scheme 24</a>). Its biosynthesis initiates with the cyclization of farnesyl diphosphate by AstA to yield (–)-daucane, followed by AstB-catalyzed oxidation to convert a methyl group to a carboxylic acid and form an epoxide. Next, the oxidation of a methyl group by AstC yields an alcohol, which facilitates an intramolecular epoxide opening to generate aspterric acid. Aspterric acid was found to inhibit DHAD at sub-micromolar levels, highlighting its capacity for use as a potent herbicidal agent. Additionally, the ability of the DHAD AstD to confer self-resistance to aspterric acid was confirmed.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="sch24"><h4 class="obj_head">Scheme 24. Biosynthesis of aspterric acid in <em>A. terreus</em>.<a href="#cit134" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit134">134</a>.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=6590338_c9md00054b-s24.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/c16d/6590338/91e42b3da05b/c9md00054b-s24.jpg" loading="lazy" height="126" width="768" alt="Scheme 24"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/sch24/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure></section><section id="sec30"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">6.2. Biosynthesis of phenguignardic acid</h3>
<p>The NRPS-like-encoding gene <em>pgnA</em> was activated in <em>A. terreus</em> using a doxycycline-dependent inducible Tet-on expression system that had previously been developed for the activation of genes in <em>A. niger</em>.<a href="#cit104" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit104">104</a>,<a href="#cit136" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit136">136</a> The system involves <em>gdpA</em>-mediated constitutive expression of the doxycycline-dependent transcriptional activator rtTA fused to <em>tetO7</em> sites and a Pmin promoter sequence that precedes the target gene. In the presence of doxycycline, rtRA binds to <em>tetO7</em>-Pmin and activates transcription of the target gene. The study revealed that activation of the <em>pgnA</em> resulted in the production of phenguignardic acid,<a href="#cit136" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit136">136</a> which has displayed non-host-specific phytotoxic activity.<a href="#cit137" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit137">137</a> Heterologous expression in <em>A. nidulans</em> confirmed that PgnA is independently responsible for phenguignardic acid production (<a href="#sch25" class="usa-link">Scheme 25</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="sch25"><h4 class="obj_head">Scheme 25. Biosynthesis of phenguignardic acid in <em>A. terreus</em>.<a href="#cit136" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit136">136</a>.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=6590338_c9md00054b-s25.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/c16d/6590338/04df055c6a1c/c9md00054b-s25.jpg" loading="lazy" height="289" width="784" alt="Scheme 25"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/sch25/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure></section><section id="sec31"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">6.3. Biosynthesis of asperphenamate</h3>
<p>The amino acid ester asperphenamate and its derivatives have displayed potent activity against breast cancer cell lines.<a href="#cit138" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit138">138</a>,<a href="#cit139" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit139">139</a> The structure of asperphenamate consists of <em>N</em>-benzoylphenylalanine and <em>N</em>-benzoylphenylalaninol subunits, which are linked together by an ester. Its bioactivity and rare structure prompted the molecular characterization of asperphenamate. Targeted gene deletions in asperphenamate-producing <em>Penicillium brevicompactum</em> confirmed that the <em>apm</em> cluster, which harbors two NRPSs and is conserved across the genomes of <em>A. terreus</em> and <em>Aspergillus aculeatus</em>, is responsible for asperphenamate biosynthesis.<a href="#cit140" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit140">140</a> The biosynthetic pathway of asperphenamate was elucidated by examining the production of biosynthetic intermediates in deletion strains and by conducting feeding studies in heterologous hosts. This study confirmed that NRPSs ApmA and ApmB are sufficient for biosynthesis of asperphenamate, despite the presence of potential tailoring enzymes within the cluster (<a href="#sch26" class="usa-link">Scheme 26</a>). First, ApmA biosynthesizes an amide intermediate from phenylalanine and benzoic acid precursors. ApmB activates the same substrates and accepts the ApmA-biosynthesized linear dipeptidyl precursor, which are combined through inter-molecular ester bond formation to generate asperphenamate. Interestingly, this was the first study to reveal a two-module NRPS system responsible for the biosynthesis of an amino acid ester.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="sch26"><h4 class="obj_head">Scheme 26. Biosynthesis of asperphenamate in <em>A. terreus</em>.<a href="#cit140" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit140">140</a>.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=6590338_c9md00054b-s26.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/c16d/6590338/1c02e364dfa4/c9md00054b-s26.jpg" loading="lazy" height="391" width="767" alt="Scheme 26"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/sch26/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure></section><section id="sec32"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">6.4. Biosynthesis of citreoviridin</h3>
<p>Citreoviridin is an ATP synthase inhibitor that has been investigated as a therapeutic agent to target breast cancer.<a href="#cit141" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit141">141</a> To identify its biosynthetic gene cluster in <em>A. terreus</em>, a resistance-gene-driven approach was utilized.<a href="#cit142" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit142">142</a> One such cluster contained the F1-ATPase β-chain-encoding CtvE, which is a subunit of the target of citreoviridin, along with genes encoding the HR-PKS CtvA, the SAM-dependent methyltransferase CtvB, the flavin-dependent monooxygenase CtvC, and the hydrolase CtvD. To investigate the biosynthetic pathway of citreoviridin, the genes within the <em>ctv</em> cluster were heterologously expressed stepwise in <em>A. nidulans</em>, which resulted in the production of intermediates and allowed a biosynthetic pathway to be proposed (<a href="#sch27" class="usa-link">Scheme 27</a>). CtvA first biosynthesizes an α-pyrone intermediate, which undergoes hydroxyl group methylation by CtvB to yield citreomontanin. Next, the citreomontanin terminal alkenes undergo isomerization to form a (17<em>Z</em>)-hexaene, which is bisepoxidated by CtvC. CtvD then catalyzes formation of the tetrahydrofuran ring, resulting in citreoviridin production.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="sch27"><h4 class="obj_head">Scheme 27. Biosynthesis of citreoviridin in <em>A. terreus</em>.<a href="#cit142" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="cit142">142</a>.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=6590338_c9md00054b-s27.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/c16d/6590338/657fc8743ce4/c9md00054b-s27.jpg" loading="lazy" height="230" width="734" alt="Scheme 27"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/sch27/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></figure></section></section><section id="sec33"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">7. Conclusion</h2>
<p>In the post-genomic era, fungal sequencing initiatives have accelerated our ability to link SMs to their biosynthetic gene clusters. Further, they have enhanced our understanding of fungal SM biosynthetic processes and the underpinning genes that define them. Such knowledge can have enormous applications for pharmaceutical production and industrial processes, as genetic engineering can be used to optimize SM production levels or to generate useful second-generation analogs. Despite the significant progress made in the past six years, many SMs that <em>Aspergillus</em> species have the capacity to produce still have not been identified or linked to their biosynthetic gene clusters, which remains true for many other fungal species. Thorough characterization of the <em>Aspergillus</em> secondary metabolome will require a combination of approaches, including the use of inducible promoters, overexpression of pathway-specific regulators, growth in various conditions, heterologous expression, and gene knockout techniques, along with the collaborative effort of the research community.</p></section><section id="sec34"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Conflicts of interest</h2>
<p>The authors declare no conflicts of interest.</p></section><section id="ack1" class="ack"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Acknowledgments</h2>
